193700788X|1.0|I didn't think that there could be a bigger cop out, in terms of an ending to a much beloved series, than the Harry Potter epilogue. I was completely wrong. This is by far the number one book on that list now. I feel totally robbed & slightly betrayed by CH. She chose the easy way out, the mundane & predictable route, for the simple reason that she could not be bothered to put any effort into this book creatively; and it shows on every single one of the book's three-hundred-odd pages. Even the most crucial of plot points came to the most laughably boring resolutions CH has ever written.<br /><br />There was no depth to any of the characters, so much so that even all the major players in the series came off almost unrecognizable to me & I consider myself a devoted fan, an extremely thorough reader & re-reader--and one that made sure to re-read the entire series right prior to the release of DEA. Yes, Sookie does come full circle: she began as a back-woods barmaid and she ends up right back where she started. I felt like all of her growth as a character and as a woman was thrown out the window in order to accommodate the ending, which literally puts the reader back at square one.<br /><br />She \\"chooses\\" Sam (AKA defaults to him) even though we all thought Sookie understood from the very beginning that mixing business with pleasure was never a wise choice. Though this notion is repeated several times throughout the series & it would seem that Sookie would abide by this adage, especially since she ends up with a much larger stake in the bar as co-owner, she apparently has absorbed none of this. Sookie's apparent desire for a man with a \\"quiet\\" mind, another notion repeated on numerous occasions, also goes out the window even though she states explicitly in previous books that she could never be with a man who's mind she can read. Her sudden inability to properly read the thoughts of shifters, even though she was perfectly capable of it in all the other books, was employed merely to accommodate the ending. CH knew the readers would never believe a Sookie that chooses Sam while a relationship with Eric is still viable so she conveniently squeezes Eric between a rock & a hard place and completely tears-to-shreds the integrity of her most imaginative character by-far, all to force the ending of the series into some farce of plausibility. Not to mention the enormous number of details regarding Sookie & Eric's relationship throughout the series that were just plain forgotten so that CH could make room for Sam...<br /><br />Almost all of this book's characterizations felt completely non sequitur in comparison to the characterizations that were premised during the overall arc of the story, especially in regard to Sookie and Eric, for whom CH didn't bother doing any justice. Their interactions in this book were more indicative of two characters that hardly knew each other and dated only casually. What happened to everything that happened between them while Eric was cursed? What happened to the blood bond and their vampire marriage? To the numerous times they saved each other's lives? Eric was hardly featured in this book and their communication almost nil, when Deadlocked revolved almost entirely around the relationship between Sookie & Eric...a relationship that has dominated the entire series--therefore the majority of Sookie's dating life--and she suddenly doesn't give a damn that Eric is marrying another woman in two weeks?!<br /><br />Sookie has never been casual about sex, yet she has sex with Sam right after her marriage is dissolved and even considers sleeping with Bill?! Sookie broke her blood bond to Eric to ensure that her feelings weren't being manipulated by magic, but somehow she can't care that the Cluviel Dor's magic manipulated into being her romantic feelings for Sam? Sookie even sounds utterly ambivalent about Sam at the end of the book, like she couldn't be bothered about whether her relationship with him will even exist in the future; and according to this final book she and Eric only had what apparently turned out to be a degenerating relationship that revolved mainly around both parties pride and mutual need for vengeance in the end. Somehow we, as your readers, are supposed to swallow all of this and believe it too? Yes CH is free to do whatever she wants with her own characters, but please don't insult the intelligence of your readers and then call us entitled for it.<br /><br />I can't believe that I actually get watery-eyed whenever I randomly catch myself thinking about this book, even though I knew what was coming since CH went with the most obvious of all her plot devices on this one--which btw, was so glaringly in-your-face during the last couple of books that I had blissfully convinced myself that it was nothing more than a red-herring since CH does like to twist things up quite often. I obviously gave the woman too much credit too soon. Absolutely no plot twists to save this one though. I'm not sure I even have the proper words to sum up everything that I'm feeling because Sookie & I have been friends for what I consider many years now, but I am ashamed to admit that the overwhelming emotion feels genuinely like grief. CH has made me feel like such an a** for investing my time, money, and emotions in these books to only have the series end this way.<br /><br />I honestly think I might hate Sookie now, which is what I believe CH wanted in the end so that she would not and could not be forced to write more in or about the Sookie-verse. I'm heartbroken because the Southern Vampire Mysteries series was one I followed lovingly for quite a length of time. I never in my wildest dreams thought I would say this, but thank goodness for the fact that True Blood does not follow the plot-line of the books. I'm literally crossing all my fingers and toes hoping that the True Blood writers will take pity on all the Eric fans & give us, as well as Eric, the kind of resolution we bothdeserve after everything we've been through together, regardless of the outcome of his relationship with Sookie.<br /><br />If I, as a reader, wanted a \\"realistic\\" human relationship as a conclusion, then I simply would not have picked up a supernatural mystery novel full of demons, faeries, werewolves, and vampires. What happened to the idea that love conquers all regardless of all the seemingly insurmountable obstacles? There is not an ounce of the magic in this book that so endeared me to the early books in this series. And to those criticizing the readers that gave strongly negative reviews about the book (including CH herself), it's not whining, it's called giving a heartfelt opinion, which is exactly what writing a review is about. The vast majority of Sookie fans have no intentions of harming CH or themselves to get her attention. Yes, the characters and story do belong to CH and she is free to use both to whatever end she wishes; but if she didn't want an intense reaction from her readers, then she should have kept these characters to herself instead of sharing them with us for more than a decade and expecting us not to have such strong opinions about them.<br /><br />CH completely expected this reception from the majority of her fans and she's getting exactly what she knew she was in for. Even though the reaction to this book was obvious to CH long beforehand, she probably couldn't fathom the reality of it--which has most likely been much worse than anything even she expected. Mrs. Harris, that is exactly how all of your devoted Sookie & Eric fans feel about the way you chose to end the series. The outcome is painfully obvious beforehand, but still unfathomable in it's reality and utterly worse than expected. Plain & simple, please cut the \\"entitled readers\\" some slack if you expect some in return.

I would never in a million years have guessed that Ray Bradbury would be singing Gene Kelly's praises, but there it was. Once the carnvial's leaders realize that the boys are suspicious of their actions, they seek to silence them by whatever means necessary: bribery, terror, or maybe even death.<br /><br />When I started reading this book (which was on Halloween...it took me forever to finish it because I kept getting distracted by other books), I posted a tweet claiming that Bradbury \\"writes like a dream.\\" I usually use this phrase to mean \\"writes so darn well.\\" In this case, that's what I meant at first. But as I progressed through the novel, I realized that Bradbury's writing was also often dreamlike: sort of hazy, yet somehow beautifully descriptive. There are soft, touching moments as well as scenes filled with some of the creepiest suspense.<br /><br />Until about two-thirds of the way through, I couldn't wait to read more of Bradbury's work. My only complaint with this book is that at the end it gets sort of preachy -- not in a religious way, more allegorical, I guess. And while I enjoy a moral that's baked into the story, I don't like when it's slapped on top like icing on a cake, sitting on top of the action like a separate entity. (Note: The message in the story is a good one; it was just too overt for me.)<br /><br />All in all: I'd read Bradbury again for sure. I just hope that his works don't all read like cautionary tales.tity. 0385349947|5.0|I'd heard bad press and good press about Sheryl Sandberg so I was almost afraid her book, \\"Lean In\\" would be an exhorting/preaching book handed down \\"from above.\\" Thankfully, it isn't. While she states at the outset what she believes -- \\"A truly equal world would be one where women ran half our countries and companies and men ran half our homes. I truly believe this would be a better world.\\" -- she is quick to admit regarding errors made climbing up the ladder, \\"This is not a list of things other women have done. I have made every mistake on this list.\\"<br /><br />When I am reviewing a book I want to give 1 or 5 stars to, I often quickly run through those reviews who would oppose mine, looking for balance. Perhaps they have seen something I missed. In this case I was shaken by the vitriol of those who not only hadn't seemed to have read the same book I did, not only hadn't noticed that the stats Sandberg quotes were not her \\"opinion,\\" but who were ragingly ugly over the fact that Sandberg is a \\"rich woman.\\" They felt she'd had it \\"easy,\\" and therefore \\"had no right\\" to tell less rich women what to do. It seems the divide between the \\"haves\\" and the \\"have nots\\" is greater than I was aware of. In my humble opinion, she does not come off as anything approaching elitist or oblivious at all.<br /><br />To be clear, I am soundly working class as were both my parents, one a factory technician and the other a waitress. I was the first person in my family to attend college and I paid for it myself by working my way through. Being a human being, I wanted to be a success. But, of course, we have a choice as to what we want to be successful in, as Sandberg points out innumerable times, especially singling out full time mothers. I've run the gauntlet of several corporate rat races, and surely do know by now that there is more to life than your job. But the jobs I came to love and be passionate about are a big part of my identity. And many of those jobs did eventually die inside me for the very fact that, as a woman, I had nowhere to go but sideways or out. This book would have been extremely helpful to me back then.<br /><br />Though \\"Lean In\\" covers serious issues, it isn't a heavy, boring tome. It is written with a light and deft hand, humor there, if understated. She laments, \\"I have breast-fed two children and noted, at times with great dissappointment, that this was simply not something my husband was equipped to do.\\"<br /><br />There has long been a spell on the world -- the spell of the myth of male superiority. If you believe that a God created us, wouldn't it be stupid and exceedingly cruel for God to make one sex superior to another? The best reason to believe in the gender equality of leadership is given thusly by Sandberg: \\"The ability to learn is the most important quality a leader can have.\\" As a former teacher I have never once noticed little girls more unable to learn than little boys.<br /><br />Sandberg takes the time to list the external factors that contributed to her success. The internal factors<br />are self-evident as you read the book. And she doesn't shy away from the thorny issues of sex. She notes that Sr. men and Jr. women avoid one another, pointing out that \\"We cannot assume that interactions between men and women have a sexual component.\\" Of course, we can't assume they don't, either, but her point is well-taken. For a more straight-forward relationship between men and women in the workplace I would think men must be willing to drop their sense of entitlement every time they get aroused. But will they? We all know that when rumors start between a woman and a man, the woman gets the worst of it, even is she's totally innocent. I could call women out, too, I suppose, and say that those who think to get ahead by using their sexual \\"wiles\\" should choose another course, one with more integrity and self-respect. But, honestly, how many women still actually do that? A negligible amount.<br /><br />It was with relief that I read Sandberg's stance on emotions -- always a tender spot between the sexes.  Women get such a bad rap for their \\"feelings.\\" States Sandberg: \\"Sharing emotions builds deeper relationships.\\" And she cites the studies to support it. But, of course, women have always known this, haven't we? Men know it, too, but are mostly unwilling to experience it for fear of being called a \\"sissy,\\" and other epithets along those lines. In fact, a few nights ago on a talk show I heard a man joke that one way a woman could know if a man was gay is how avid he was about watching \\"Glee.\\" I got news for him. Any man who likes music or is a musician, who has issues with high school, who likes good storylines and who is at ease with his masculinity and emotions, likes \\"Glee.\\"<br /><br />Sandberg also does a good job dissecting the \\"Queen Bee\\" syndrome. She points out that, \\"It makes no sense for women to feel that we are competing against one another anymore,\\" and goes on to urge women to help and support one another. It wasn't within the scope of this book, but I'd like to point out that ten thousand years plus of being essentially worthless except as you may serve a man, and literally being \\"owned\\" by men can have, shall we say, a deleterious effect on a woman's outlook. Competing with other women for a man was the only survival technique women were allowed. By now it's in our DNA. I hope that need will die soon as we build a more equitable society and workplace.<br /><br />As Sandberg says, \\"Until women have supportive employers and colleagues as well as partners who share family responsibilities, they don't have a real choice. And until men are fully respected for contributing inside the home, they don't have a real choice either.\\"<br /><br />However, why must success be gotten in this either/or way? Why must women be as aggressive and hard-charging as men? Women could (and should) do it differently. It appears that Sandberg may be thinking this way. She writes, \\" . . . in business we are taught to fit in, but I was starting to think this might not be the right approach.\\" Amen!<br /><br />It is said that those who give much, who are nurturers, tend to develop more depth than those who don't, or who tend to be on the receiving end. Sandberg seems to agree, saying \\"So perhaps one positive result of having more women at the top is that our leaders will have been trained to care more about the well-being of others. My hope, of course, is that we won't have to play by these archaic rules forever and that eventually we can all be ourselves.\\"<br /><br />Buy the book and give it to a graduating high school senior. Either gender.f that I read Sandberg's stance on emotions -- always a tender spot between the sexes.  Women get such a bad rap for their \\"feelings.\\" States Sandberg: \\"Sharing emotions builds deeper relationships.\\" And she cites the studies to support it. But, of course, women have always known this, haven't we? Men know it, too, but are mostly unwilling to experience it for fear of being called a \\"sissy,\\" and other epithets along those lines. In fact, a few nights ago on a talk show I heard a man joke that one way a woman could know if a man was gay is how avid he was about watching \\"Glee.\\" I got news for him. Any man who likes music or is a musician, who has issues with high school, who likes good storylines and who is at ease with his masculinity and emotions, likes \\"Glee.\\"<br /><br />Sandberg also does a good job dissecting the \\"Queen Bee\\" syndrome. She points out that, \\"It makes no sense for women to feel that we are competing against one another anymore,\\" and goes on to urge women to help and support one another. It wasn't within the scope of this book, but I'd like to point out that ten thousand years plus of being essentially worthless except as you may serve a man, and literally being \\"owned\\" by men can have, shall we say, a deleterious effect on a woman's outlook. Competing with other women for a man was the only survival technique women were allowed. By now it's in our DNA. I hope that need will die soon as we build a more equitable society and workplace.<br /><br />As Sandberg says, \\"Until women have supportive employers and colleagues as well as partners who share family responsibilities, they don't have a real choice. And until men are fully respected for contributing inside the home, they don't have a real choice either.\\"<br /><br />However, why must success be gotten in this either/or way? Why must women be as aggressive and hard-charging as men? Women could (and should) do it differently. It appears that Sandberg may be thinking this way. She writes, \\" . . . in business we are taught to fit in, but I was starting to think this might not be the right approach.\\" Amen!<br /><br />It is said that those who give much, who are nurturers, tend to develop more depth than those who don't, or who tend to be on the receiving end. Sandberg seems to agree, saying \\"So perhaps one positive result of having more women at the top is that our leaders will have been trained to care more about the well-being of others. My hope, of course, is that we won't have to play by these archaic rules forever and that eventually we can all be ourselves.\\"<br /><br />Buy the book and give it to a graduating high school senior. Either gender.

B009NY43NY|2.0|If you are like me, you might not be sure you want to read/purchase this book given the entire slew of awful reviews. It really is hard to sort through the anger and the bitterness and venom circulating, to find reliable opinions about whether this book is any good or not.<br /><br />My mother in law purchased it for her Nook, so after she went to bed I borrowed the nook and sat down to read. It took about four hours.<br /><br />The most awful line of the book: He looked more prosperous than when he was broke.  - Really? You don't say!<br />The best line of the book: \\"Sookie. You are mine.\\"<br /><br />I didn't like the non-Sookie Pov sections, and honestly I don't think they added to the story. If anything I think they only served as filler to lengthen the book. Which is truly sad, because in all actuality most of the book felt like filler. I was never able to settle into a cohesive plot because it seemed like each page was a new episode of \\"who will show up for a brief reconciliation from their past wrongs today.\\" It felt like each character that has been important in the past that was still alive or undead showed up with an apology for how they'd wronged her in the past, only to help her for a scene, then be called away. The ones who couldn't come make a half-hearted apology followed by an excuse of why she isn't first to them, participated in the juvenile plot against her.<br /><br />Eric isn't as much as shadow of himself as some of the reviews would lead you to be. We still get a \\"Sookie, you are mine.\\" He does absolutely everything in his power to protect Sookie, even at the expense of himself. This, to me, was the best part of the book. I've known for so long that he does truly love her, and I think many of his actions showed it.  While I don't agree with all of his actions, and some things were disappointing, I think their resolutions could have been carried out better.<br /><br />After his trouble in the previous book, Sam truly is a shadow of himself in this volume. I saw nothing of the close friendship between them of the past, and he left me boggled a lot of the time. He did put Sookie first, and I suppose that should count for something, but his choice didn't seem reasonable or in character.<br /><br />I've attempted to keep the first part of this review as spoiler free as possible. If you don't want spoilers, please don't read any more of this review.<br /><br />As far as Sookie and Eric not ending up together: it breaks my heart, but I think it could have been done in a way that I would have been able to accept. The reasons given (that he was determined to turn her, and she didn't want that) are valid and do make sense. But to be effective, it needed to be set up better. IF they weren't going to have a HEA together ( and I really wish they would have ) then I think they should have ended it a few books ago. They could have broken up, and then in book twelve they could have found a way to be friends, much like her and bill. They could have found a happily not together, but not out of each other's lives. Would I have been sad? Yes. But I would have understood. Then, the romance between her and Sam could have been a big undercurrent of book 12. We could have seen chemistry growing strongly, and even possible have been routing for them to wake up and realize it. Then she could have saved him in the end, we would cross our fingers that it would make a turning point, and this book could have clinched it ( as long as he was more present in it. )<br /><br />I entirely respect that Charlaine Harris had her own vision of how it should end. She is the author, after all. What I don't respect is the way it was carried out. She could have had this end, while still offering fans a sense of gratification for sticking with the series for 13 books.<br /><br />In conclusion, if you are still on the fence about whether to read this or not, I would say it honestly doesn't matter. I don't think it offers any satisfying conclusion foranything. Even Sam and Sookie aren't a done deal. If you are looking for closure, maybe the coda will offer it. If you want to read it just so you will have read everything, then go for it.anything. Even Sam and Sookie aren't a done deal. If you are looking for closure, maybe the coda will offer it. If you want to read it just so you will have read everything, then go for it.

The debate here was very fierce and combative as almost an debate in American history.  It was as hard hitting and as personal as almost anything we see today. B000FC13Y0|5.0|A foolish boy, Tristan, makes a romantic promise to fetch a fallen star for the girl he has a crush on. Then he actually tries to go get it from beyond the \\"wall\\" where there's a fairy land like no other.<br /><br />Gaiman breaks all kinds of rules in Stardust. (As an \\"emerging\\" novelist myself, I've been studying these rules). The story starts with the hero's very conception! That's something so David-Copperfield-antiquated that a modern writer rarely attempts it. Stardust also has quite a bit of summary,  a.k.a. telling instead of showing--eek!.<br /><br />However, it also has a lot of tried and true storytelling elements: a great hook, some fabulously nasty villains and a \\"quest\\" plot where the hero goes in search of an object to help him win true love. The hero gets what he wants but not in the way you might expect--all good stuff.<br /><br />But by far the greatest reason Stardust is such fun to read--and I'm beginning to suspect this is true for most of Gaiman's books--is the quality and originality of its imagined world. So much fantasy these days just rips off a page from Tolkien's world or a traditional fairy tale, but Gaiman manages to make Stardust's magical world feel familiar while at the same time creating characters and situations that are quite different.<br /><br />So check Stardust out. I highly recommend it. Oh and the movie adaptation isn't bad either...<br />(This review and others can also be found on my blog at sarazaske.wordpress.com)

B001AVCFJM|2.0|Despite the fact that there are over 1800 reviews for 2009's STAR TREK on here already, I feel compelled to add my opinion that this movie is a big disappointment. OK, it's got good production values, with nice special effects and a good cast. I'm not surprised that it's popular with most younger non-Star Trek fans. But the best of all the Star Trek incarnations have had good, thoughtful stories giving us a hopeful future for humanity. What is the basic story in this movie? A renegade Romulan is trying to destroy the planet Vulcan by sinking a black hole into it, and the Enterprise has to stop him. From that point on it's over-the-top action with no positive vision for the future at all. Forget about interesting science fiction concepts! Some people have said that the story of this movie does not matter much since it's primarily establishing the characters for future movies. But what good are characters if there's no interesting story? Not much.<br /><br />Further, the glaring inconsistencies with previous Star Treks makes this movie unacceptable to me as a long-time fan of the show. Other reviewers have gone over much of this, but the ones that are most glaring to me are 1)most of the original cast being on the Enterprise under Captain Pike, 2)Ensign Chekov being navigator under Captain Pike when he took that role in the original Trek's second season under Captain Kirk, 3)Spock's mother dying when Spock is a young officer under Pike. What about \\"Journey to Babel\\" from the original series (TOS)? 4)Spock saying \\"not this time\\" when Kirk suggests being compassionate to the Romulans, 5)Spock and Uhura having a hot romance going (sorry, \\"Amok Time\\" from TOS is ignored too), and 6)the design of the bridge and other parts of the Enterprise looking nothing like it did in TOS's pilot episode, \\"The Cage\\" when Pike was captain.<br /><br />Supposedly these discrepancies with earlier Star Treks are explained by Nero's destruction of the timeline, thus creating a parallel universe that we see in the movie, but that too is inconsistent with Star Trek's use of time in past series and movies, and also many of these discrepancies exist before Nero's actions or would not be explained by a timeline change. To explain what I mean by it being an inconsistency, in the TOS episode \\"City On the Edge of Forever\\" Star Trek's concept of time is made clear when McCoy went back to the past and makes a substantial change, which caused the future to vanish. His actions did NOT create an alternate universe as happens in this movie. I don't believe that anyone understands time well enough to say which result would actually happen if we could travel back in time and make some big change, but this movie's concept of time is NOT the established Star Trek concept of time. As far as I know, and I DON'T know all the Star Trek episodes of the more recent TV series, I believe that Star Trek has adhered to that basic concept of time as set forth in the original series. Until 2009's STAR TREK.<br /><br />At any rate, even if I am wrong that this movie violates Star Trek's established use of time, should fans accept these huge changes to Star Trek history, even IF this movie's explanation of those changes makes sense? I say NO!! I speculate that the director and writers and producer of this movie really didn't want to make a \\"Star Trek\\" movie AT ALL, yet they HAD to because they were commissioned to make a Star Trek movie. Therefore, they took a basic Star Trek background then went off in their own direction.<br /><br />Some other aspects of this movie also bother me. For one, I agree with the reviewers who see it as absurd that Kirk is made captain of the Enterprise at the end of this movie. Not only is he depicted here as impulsive and arrogant, but he has no experience as an officer and has not even graduated from Star Fleet Academy! Another scene that I found to be unreasonable was the one where Spock nearly strangled Kirk to death on the bridge. After such an incident of out-of-control rage, Spock should have been removed as captain. In the original Star Trek series, the only time that Spock displayed such a lack of self-control (in \\"Amok Time,\\" \\"This Side of Paradise,\\" and \\"All Our Yesterdays\\") was when he was under the influence of forces beyond his control. Another problem with the scene where Spock nearly strangled Kirk to death is that a large group of crew members just STOOD THERE AND WATCHED!! Finally, Spock's father calmly says, \\"Spock\\" and everyone lets Spock decide whether he's going to kill Kirk or not. I can understand the dramatic desire to have Spock make the decision of what he will do, but it's not reasonable that all those people would just stand there and let him kill Kirk.<br /><br />The next Trek movie is coming out in about a week, again directed by J.J. Abrams as this one was. Unless I hear good explanations that it's a lot better than 2009's STAR TREK, I'm not going to go see it. 193700788X|1.0|I'm so glad I didn't preorder this book. I've bought and read (and reread) the first 11 books and always looked forward to each new book. I lost interest after the 11th book but was going to get the last two just to see the whole series through. After reading the reviews, I know not to waste my time and money. I understand that it's a writers prerogative to write her characters as she sees fits, but it's also a writer's responsibility to be consistent within the framework she has created. You can take a 300 pound couch potato who wants to be a ballerina and transform her into one over the course of a series, but you can't have her be a couch potato almost the whole time and then suddenly turn her into a prima ballerina at the very end without explanation. (Silly example, I know, but you get my point). It doesn't make sense and it's just bad writing. I'm grateful to Charlaine Harris for creating these wonderful characters, I just wish she had given them an ending that was consistent with the rest of the storyline.

193700788X|1.0|Such an epic disappointment. Not that I expected much after the last couple of books, but I thought CH might bring out the big guns for the final book. Looks like she dragged out a water pistol.<br /><br />*****SPOILER ALERT*****<br /><br />Yes, I'm an Eric fan and I would have liked to see them somehow have their HEA...But...I am reasonable enough to realise that that probably wasn't going to happen. First, let me say that I've read hundreds of these reviews. I truly wanted to see if the way I was feeling was just me or the general feeling amongst the fan base. What I've found and what really annoys me is the presumption by some of the 5 star fans (and they've actually mentioned it) that we're all bitching about Eric. NO, we're not. Again and again (ex)fans are saying the same thing...the gist of which is that while it's disappointing that Sookie didn't end up with Eric, we could have accepted it if there had been ANY logical build up to Sam. We're saying that the book was sloppily thrown together; that CH had an ending in mind at around book 2 and so-help-her she was ending it that way even if the story and characters had really found a voice of their own and diverged from that ending...she was going to finagle, forget continuity, change lore, anything to get her ending; that the premise/mystery was poorly thought out and that CH threw anyone that had ever been in the books into the mix to act as filler with no real need for them to be there (especially as she's supposedly letting us know all their ever-afters in the coda out in October). I won't go into all the errors in the book (because so many other reviewers have) but I have to seriously question her publisher, continuity specialist and editor. How did all the errors get passed them? Did they even read it before sending it to print?<br /><br />I'm incredibly disappointed that there was no real, mature and emotional \\"ending\\" for Eric and Sookie. OK, so they couldn't be together...so let them have a mature discussion and have them realise that, say their goodbyes, voice their regrets...goddamn it...just a bloody hug would have been nice (no pun intended)....but noooo, we have an emotionally immature reaction from Sookie (and a totally out of character threatening, sulky attitude from Eric) and a slamming door. Real mature *snort*. Oh yes, I can certainly see how Sookie has matured through the series, NOT! And no, I'm not saying Eric doesn't have his faults, and realistically Sookie probably needed a \\"man\\" who could live with her in the sun, father children etc, but I'd rather have seen her alone than settling for Sam. Because settle she did. Nice safe, boring Sam.<br /><br />CH has made no bones about the fact that she's never understood the fans love of Eric. What I can't understand is that she created and wrote him as a heroic, but flawed, bad-boy character, with dazzling good looks, a ready smirk and grin, a sense of humour, a real presence, and a habit of taking bullets for Sookie. He never hid his true nature. What you saw, is what you got. Yes, he hid vampire related information from Sookie but at least he always told her the truth when he could. And lets not forget the \\"gracious plenty\\" and what he could do with it. What did she expect us to do? What's not to like? Sookie fell in love with him, why wouldn't we?<br /><br />After the promise of the first 8/9 books, the progress Sookie made in accepting herself and making an effort to grow and learn, it was sad to see what many thought of as such a promising heroine close herself off to possibilities. To slide back into her mundane little existence and her mundane little job; to a relationship with someone who never gave her the credit due her intelligence, treated her like a child and generally took her a bit for granted. But in saying that, I never really liked Sookie all that much anyway. I never saw what ever Eric, Alcide, Quinn and Bill did. I found her childish, self-centred, whiney, ungrateful, self-righteous,hypocritical and sanctimonious. I could go on...and on. Probably the only redeeming qualities I found in her, were her loyalty and tolerance...but even those qualities became questionable towards the end. On a side note - I also have to wonder about CH's ideas on rape. How could she have Sookie forgive, be friendly with and even kiss her rapist????? Bit of a slap in the face for victims everywhere. Just saying!<br /><br />I only continued reading the series (and paying out good money) because of the developing relationship with Eric. He's the one who enticed me to keep reading the series. He's probably the greatest character she'll ever create and she destroyed him (and in my mind Sookie as well). So a big boo to Ms Harris for her treatment of him in the last book and for the piss-poor writing effort that so obviously showed to me that she didn't really care after about book 9 or so. I can even understand her wanting to move on/feeling resentment if she felt forced (contractually obligated) to continue writing after a certain point...but she should have done her best to give us, her readers and paying customers, the best she had to offer...not because we expected it, or deserved it, or whatever...but because she's supposed to be a professional. But, alas, it was not to be. I'm never picking up another book of hers again. Wouldn't risk having another series of hers end in the same disappointment. Find your HEA in fanfic. There's some fantastic, well written, well thought out stories... and they won't cost you a thing!<br /><br />ONE FINAL WORD<br /><br />This is quoted from a reader on the Sookieverse site...and I think it sums up everything...<br /><br />\\"In my fictional world - you don't settle for tomatoes, creeps like ALO don't win, rapists don't make good friends, love is worth fighting for, every day is a new possibility, the most meaningful acceptance you can find is within yourself, what makes you different is embraced and it is never wrong to follow your heart or hope for MORE. B00AGJ4HC2|4.0|A member of my strategy game group broke this out recently, as a quick filler to end the night. We thought it was horribly dumb...and we couldn't stop laughing while playing it. While not my usual type of game, it was a lot of fun.<br /><br />In Love Letter, you're trying to get a letter to the princess. Your letter will be passed from person to person; when the deck runs out, whoever has the princess (or the person closest to her) wins the round. The round can (and often does) also end early if all but one player is out.<br /><br />Play is simple. You have one card in your hand, draw another card, and play one of the two. Each card has a special ability, such as letting you look at someone else's hand, or comparing your hand with someone else's (where whoever has the lower card is out of the round). There's a little bit of thinking involved, as you try to guess what cards everyone else holds so that you can choose your targets correctly, but it's largely luck-based. It wouldn't work at all as a longer game, but for a humorous filler it's pretty decent. I'm not sure how it would stand up to more plays, though.<br /><br />Aside from the cards, the game comes with a nice bag and an instruction booklet that largely consists of backstory explaning how the cards go together.

0547564651|5.0|This topic, like Timothy Noah's The Great Divergence and Charles Murray's Coming Apart, addresses some fundamental issues in our American society, including why the poor often continues to struggle.  Those of us who live comfortably in the middle-class (or above), may feel far removed from some of the struggles that the author Paul Tough (love that last name!) documents.  Whether close or near now, they are problems that ultimately will affect us all -- directly or indirectly.<br /><br />This book is not a how-to manual for parents of naughty children, so I understand why some readers were disappointed.  (I considered taking off a point for its somewhat misleading marketing and title, but decided not to, because I think the topic and Tough's ideas are so important.)  Instead, it is an important work on how to raise a successful generation of poor kids.  It shows how complex and intractable the problems of the poor can be, but it also points to meaningful steps that can be taken for success.  Tough takes a middle approach to his diagnosis, seeing it as a multidimensional challenge that is ultimately a matter of character-building.<br /><br />I teach at a suburban community college, so I do see a pretty wide range of students -- all ages, races, and socioeconomic circumstances. It gave me a greater appreciation for the challenges that some of us face.<br /><br />I recommend this book as a policy prescription -- not a self-help tome.

B007SGM084|2.0|Or why reading these books may give you horrible Twilight flashbacks...<br /><br />A quick synopsis... Anastasia Steele is a bookish 22 year old graduating college and trying to find a job in the publishing world.  Christian Gray is a 28 year self-made business tycoon.  Fifty Shades of Gray was originally written as Twilight fan-fic, but then recast with original characters.<br /><br />First, let me begin by saying I read a lot... an almost psychotic amount.  Given a choice, I'll read 3 books a day when I'm busy with life and 6-8 books a day when I'm bored.  Therefore, it's no surprise that when the hulabaloo around Fifty started, I was bound to read the entire trilogy, because I needed something to do one morning.  It surprised me when I read them, read them again, and again.  I read them repeatedly for 4 days.  I learned a lot and I was disappointed a lot.  We'll start with the good things...<br /><br />The books are funny.  Author E.L. James has a quirky writing style that's entertaining.  It's a very relaxed style and James does an outstanding job writing an easily-read book, a book with comfortable prose that is neither banal or mediocre.  Although she occasionally uses words that the average non-reader may need to look up in a dictionary, the context is descriptive.  Using a $50 word in $10 prose isn't easy to do without sounding pompous or condescending, but James does it elegantly almost every time.<br /><br />The trilogy is a credible story arc.  James provides a snapshot of time in the characters' lives, but seamlessly weaves details of their pasts so you end with a true understanding of why these people are as they are.<br /><br />Inevitability isn't a given, although sometimes you can plainly see the next step. While the reader can see a pattern about mid-way of Book 2, there are still twists so the obvious path isn't always the one taken.  That fact alone makes this trilogy worth reading.<br /><br />It continues to be interesting even to the last word of the last book.  Each little cliff hanger leaves you wanting just enough to continue reading.  Since the set is a trilogy, I recommend reading the first 5 chapters.  If you like where it's going, just buy the other 2 books.  When you get to the end of Book 1, you won't want to pause long enough to download it from Amazon, much less drive to a book store.<br /><br />The books might help your sex life.  Whether that's with a partner or alone, they will definitely add a few shades of interesting.  The sex writing is well done.  I'll let you explore why it's well done.<br /><br />Now, the bad...<br /><br />The characters are typical.  She's bookish, he's charming.  She's middle-class, he's rich.  She's younger and virginal, he's older and worldly.  Sadly, the banality of her characters overrides her wonderful, quirky writing.  Each time Anastasia has a \\"wow golly gee\\" moment, I rolled my eyes.  Each time Christian has a \\"trust me baby\\" moment, I wanted to roll his eyes like dice.  Much of the character exposition could have been dubbed by any of the adults in Charlie Brown movies.<br /><br />This book could have been called Fifty Shades of Fake Sex and been accurate.  The sex depicted is a fantasy and not always in the good way. Before the first sex scene, I knew this was written by a woman.  By the time the BDSM sex started, I also knew this was written by someone who's not part of the BDSM lifestyle.  She should have done her homework.  The sex read like a horny teenage girl and that's only interesting to other horny teenage girls.  It will pull you in, just don't expect reality to look anything like this.  It's porn for women.<br /><br />There's too much sex; she substitutes quantity for quality.  I don't care that they're in love and they're 20-somethings in good health with loads of energy.  It's not real and it's not even close sometimes.  She doesn't try to make the sex timing feel real and that ruins large sections of the book.  Again, it's writing for a hornyteenage girl.  The well-written sex descriptions are killed by the unrealistic timing and bad research.<br /><br />Christian's back story paints his BDSM lifestyle as the result of child abuse.  For those that understand the BDSM lifestyle, that's simply not true.  BDSM isn't the result of anything other than a natural curiosity about the psychology of sex and adult sexual interaction.  James had the opportunity to build a bridge of understanding between the Average and BDSM worlds.  She failed spectacularly.<br /><br />The ending is predictable.  James worked so hard in the beginning to take the story down unexpected paths and then she gives it a storybook ending.  While I saw it coming, I kept hoping I was wrong.  The storybook ending adds to the bad fantasy aspect of the trilogy.  There were so many other endings, some happy and some not, that James could have chosen.  Her elegant writing remains elegant to the end, but her genius is greatly diminished by the trite ending.<br /><br />The psychological basis for Dominance/submission play is too one-sided from the female perspective.  Christian's psychological exposition needs a lot of work.  Again, the female writer didn't do enough research on the male psyche, especially one as damaged as Christian's.  I found myself appreciative of his willingness to improve himself, but the time frame of the story made his change unbelievable.  Anastasia's psychological exposition was better.  The very short time frame was on the edge of suspect, but still plausible.<br /><br />I hate to end on a bad note, so...<br /><br />Despite Anastasia's \\"golly gee\\" and Christian's \\"trust me, baby,\\" the character interaction is well done.  Kudos for an interesting set of books.  Learn from your mistakes and keep writing.  Despite my unhappiness with these books, I sincerely look forward to the next one.

B00BPWDMP2|3.0|Country trio Lady Antebellum have sculpted a fine career for themselves over the year.  After multiple Grammys and continued commercial success, the trio haven't attempted to shake up their sound because, honestly, why should they? \\"If Ain't Broke, don't fix it\\" right?  Because of this philosophy, [[ASIN:B00BPWDMP2 Golden]] is merely a good album, but not an exciting or innovative one.  This is an album that should be 'just what the doctor ordered' to Lady A fans, but to more casual listeners, may not strike an earth-shattering chord. The standard cues are always in place: beautiful vocal blend between Charles Kelley and Hillary Scott and alternation of the lead, songwriting/guitar contributions from Dave Haywood, and somewhat conservative, thoughtful production work.<br /><br />\\"Get To Me\\" opens solidly, but it isn't exceptionally engaging.  It does the 'expected', but just leaves the desire to take things up a notch.  \\"Goodbye Town\\" falls into a similar boat, this time with Charles Kelly handling lead duties.  The biggest detraction with \\"Goodbye Town\\" is that it feels as if it will never end.  At the end, Kelley is given too much free time to ad lib unnecessarily.  Things look up with the proceeding trio of \\"The First Time\\", \\"Downtown\\" and \\"Better Off Now (That You're Gone)\\", which are stronger.  Sure, the formula remains much the same, but all three cuts seem to have more polish than either \\"Get To Me\\" or \\"Goodbye Town\\" did.<br /><br />\\"It Ain't Pretty\\" is pretty good, summed up by lyric \\"It ain't pretty when a heart breaks\\", but still it doesn't ruffle many feathers.  \\"Can't Stand The Rain\\" remains conservative in sound, though grows in intensity on the chorus.  \\"With arms open wide / I'll be your umbrella when you just can't stand the rain...\\", Kelly/Scott sing on the chorus.  It's a path already tread and somewhat schmaltzy, but its pleasant enough and the band seems sincere.  \\"Golden\\" proves to be a lovely title track, only to be followed up by a less memorable \\"Long Teenage Goodbye\\".  The problem with \\"Long Teenage Goodbye\\" is that there is not enough 'attitude' from Scott; perhaps slightly more exciting and assertive vocals could've made the track a more memorable one.  \\"All For Love\\" like many cuts is good, not breathtaking while penultimate cut \\"Better Man\\" atones somewhat, even if the theme seems recycled many times over.  Closer \\"Generation Away\\" has many things going for it, including is rhythmic nature and the use of organ, But things get a bit corny with an unnecessary lift of spiritual \\"He's Got The Whole World in His Hands\\"; it just misses the mark a bit.<br /><br />Ultimately, [[ASIN:B00BPWDMP2 Golden]] is an album that die-hard Lady Antebellum fans will find as enjoyable as the trios others albums. To those less devoted or invested in the trio, Golden is more average. I f[[ASIN:B002WIDRM6 Need You Now]] is considered the trio's last 'home run'  [[ASIN:B0050CJNJ2 Own The Night]] was good, but not as strong as it. Golden falls inline with Own The Night, but may is arguably even less memorable. There's nothing wrong with averageness, but Lady Antebellum may want to invest in more engaging and exciting material in the future.  3 stars.ble \\"Long Teenage Goodbye\\". 0812982622|4.0|Set in today's North Korea of totalitarian 'doublethink', Orphan is an absorbing, fast-paced, intricately plotted, picaresque novel.  In it, Johnson explores personal self-identity's crucial need for a supporting context of family and friends.  (Freud would say the ego has to be located in the superego.)  But the North Korean government claims that the state replaces family and friends; Dear Leader is all that anyone needs, the complete superego.  In its themes, Orphan is in many ways similar to Orwell's \\"1984,\\" but its reality in a real country much in the news today is riveting, adding a frisson of horror which is both terrifying and titillating.  The novel is extremely clever--  it's on the plausible edge of  'magical realism'.<br /><br />In Orphan's haunting peek-a-boo of shifting identities, the men are fleshed out much more realistically than the women.  Essentially all the female characters are presented through the eyes of the male characters.  Johnson tops up the hero's hunger for family and friends with a driving love interest, an actual breathing woman.  It's almost as if Johnson is preparing the screenplay: Ivanhoe, with the damsel's pennon next his heart, battles the Black Knight for her favors.  Johnson is, at the same time, continually mocking the medieval romance that he's peddling here.  After all, books have to sell if writers are going to survive.  Orwell did much the same with Julia in \\"1984:\\" she was \\"a rebel from the waist downwards.\\"  However, the love story is a rather late development, providing a tidy closure to the complex and sprawling story.  The brutal police-state action through much of the earlier narrative seems likely to sustain more male readers than female ones through to the concluding chapters.

1439127727|5.0|I have  no idea what possessed me to read this book since I rarely read nonfiction and have minimal interest in politics. I'm so glad I read this. From the first page, this look at the relationships among ex-presidents and the current president from Truman on up was fascinating. I had no idea how much Eisenhower had influenced the actions of presidents that came after him, nor how deep Nixon's tentacles entwined into even the Clinton presidency. This is a fun read with lots of detail about the egos, maneuvering and behind-the-scenes actions involved in the presidency.<br /><br />Since I don't know tons about politics, I'm not sure if all of this information will be as new to others as it was to me--but it seems that much of it did not come out until the presidents involved passed away.<br /><br />What was most memorable is how the bond created by being president reaches across party lines so that some of the closest bonds are formed between former \\"enemies\\" (George H.W. Bush and Bill Clinton being the prime example). I was left with a sense of admiration for all presidents and for the office, awe for the intellect required of all presidents, and feeling heartened that there are people who really do have the nation's best interests at heart.<br /><br />This book also shook many of my previously held beliefs about certain presidents. For example, as a staunch Democrat, I assumed George W. Bush retreated to private life so rapidly because he was hiding in shame, but based on this book, it's really because he didn't want to get in Obama's way. 030758836X|5.0|I can't believe how long I had this book sitting in my \\"to be read\\" pile.  If I had known how good it is, I'd have started reading it the day I brought it home.  It's a story about a woman's disappearance under suspicious circumstances and that is one layer of the story.  But it is also a story about two people and a marriage and that is a whole other layer.  Just when you think you know what makes them tick, the author throws another curveball and you have to re-think your assumptions. The pace and details of the book are really well done.<br /><br />I love mysteries and thrillers and this book, though different from most of the standard mysteries or thrillers I read, kept me riveted. The book is written from two different first person perspectives -- Nick and Amy. Nick's part starts in the present and Amy's part starts in the past.<br /><br />It's hard to explain why you like a book when nearly everything you can think of to say what you liked about it would spoil the suspense for people who haven't read it yet.  So I'll just give two pieces of advice -- or cautions.<br /><br />1st caution:  Do not skip ahead even one chapter while reading.  I mean it.  Even if you think you know what's going on and just want to check it out.  Every time there was a new twist or turn (and there are a lot of them!), I'd want to skip ahead.  But I resisted the urge and am very glad I did because it would have ruined everything.<br /><br />2nd caution:  Don't start this book with expectations.  It is a psychological thriller.  Most of the reviews from people who hated this book are because it didn't meet their expectations, especially their expectations for an ending that would make them happy. 1742200508|4.0|This is a travel book with a little bit of everything, and if your budget allows for only one book, you will likely survive your trip with this one.  It covers a great deal of what you need to know to scratch the surface of all of England.<br /><br />The first section is the Plan Your Trip section.  It covers the 20 Top Experiences, as well as the standard things you have to know for day-to-day life in a new place.  Money, travel tips, weather, etiquette; the basics are here.  For interests like \\"Royal England\\" or \\"Art Galleries\\", you'll find top six (or so) lists here.  Top events by month are listed, and several itineraries and walking trips cover everything from short walks to long tours.  There's a short Travel with Children section, and three paragraph summaries of each region.<br /><br />On the Road is the next and largest section.  London and Wessex get about 90 pages each, while all the other regions get 45-65 pages each.  There are maps, lists of best places to eat and stay, and sections on sights, tours, festivals & events, sleeping, eating, drinking, entertainment, and shopping.  For each region there's a bit on \\"Why Go?\\" and \\"When to Go,\\" and some history.<br /><br />This is where all the detailed information is, and this is where I searched for many things I didn't find.  I am certainly not an expert on England, yet many of my favorite things to do, pubs, restaurants, shops, and not just my favorite hotel but the whole area (with many hotels and restaurants) could not be found by searching the book or the index.  You will find all the standard tourist attractions, all the celebrity chefs' restaurants, the St. Pancras Renaissance and the easyHotel, but heck if I can find the pubs that are still owned by the locals or any of my favorite unique bookstores.<br /><br />Obviously every hotel and shop can't be included.  The book weighs almost a pound and a half as it is, and the text is small enough to give me a headache.  But while it is an excellent overview, I'd suggest carrying another book along with this one, with detailed information about the specific area you're in.  Or at the very least plan to get on the good side of some of the locals and ask them what their favorite restaurants are and where they go on their days off.  This book will guide you to the usual tourist attractions and the museums and castles you've seen in movies and TV shows (except, oddly, Highclere Castle, home of Downton Abbey), but if you want to see more of England than you've seen on the screen, you're going to need more in-depth information than can be found here.<br /><br />The next section is Understand England, with a page or ten for England Today, History, The English Table, Architecture in England, The English Landscape, The Arts, and Sporting England.  There are glossaries, a historical timeline, a few color photos, and references to websites for more information.<br /><br />At the end is the Survival Guide, a short chapter with a bit more information about the basic practicalities that were hinted at in the Plan Your Trip section.  If you've never been there, it's a good idea to read this section while actually planning your trip, to know about things like which power adapter to get and what kind of clothing to pack.<br /><br />The maps are OK, if a little difficult on the eyes.  The color maps in the London chapter are easier to read than the maps in the rest of the book.  The pull-out London map covers only central London and Hyde Park, and includes 10 Top Sights and a map of key bus routes and the Underground.  If you're planning to use buses or the Tube, pick up a better map at the stations.<br /><br />This would be a good first book to use to start planning a trip, and it really is an excellent overview of darn near everything.  But it lacks depth (as it must, given its size and the large subject it covers.)  Start here, but add other sources of information if you want to do more than scratch the surface of this wonderful country.ntry.

1742200508|5.0|I've always been a fan of Lonely Planet travel guidebooks, especially back when my wife and I traveled to exotic, off-the-beaten-path destinations worldwide once or twice a year. The Lonely Planet books we used were great at steering us in the directions of things we were interested in seeing and doing. I have no use for guidebooks with page after page of pretty color pictures. I don't care much about pictures. I always wonder how many days the photographer waited to get perfect picture-postcard shots, or how much Photoshopping it took. I prefer thick guidebooks filled with dense, authoritative text, written by people who really know the areas. And maps--guidebooks are useless without maps. Lonely Planet's \\"England,\\" 7th Edition, is an outstanding example of exactly what I look for in a travel guidebook.<br /><br />This compact, tightly bound volume is thick--848 pages, an inch and a half. It's heavy--almost two pounds. And it's densely filled with small text in two-column format. It's a superb practical guide to England (NOT including Ireland, of course, nor Wales, Scotland or Northern Ireland, which, as I understand it, are part of Great Britain but not of England proper). There are almost no photos in the main text. Color photos do appear in the 12-page introductory section \\"20 Top Experiences,\\" and in the \\"Understand England\\" section starting on Page 763. That's about it for pictures. The rest of the volume is information. Lots of information--an almost overwhelming amount of information. Fortunately, it's very well organized, and about as user-friendly as it can get considering the vast quantity of material. Chapter headings, titles and sidebars stand out in eye-soothing shades of pale blue, which also highlight features on the more than 60 useful maps interspersed within the text. Of course, the savvy visitor will also pick up some of the fantastic Ordnance Survey maps of the country--they're the best maps you can buy. Blue tabs demark the sections keyed to 12 specific regions of England. This is the meat of the book, with more than 700 pages filled to bursting with comprehensive data, including phone numbers and websites, on attractions, lodging, restaurants, events and festivals, shopping, etc. As with all guidebooks, of course, some of this material may be out-of-date or flat wrong, but you certainly can't quibble about its breadth, depth and completeness. Since my last visit to England was several years ago, I can't judge the currency or accuracy of this material. As always, the prudent traveler will call in advance or seek other resources on-line before setting off.<br /><br />England offers so much to see and do that no single travel guidebook can possibly address every visitor's interests. Lonely Planet's \\"England\\" is no exception. I looked up in the 16-page index places I visited on prior trips, and many of them were not listed--the Battle of Britain Memorial Flight, the National Motor Museum, the Tank Museum, the Fleet Air Arm Museum and the Bass Brewery, for example. If you're into museums, you should take along a separate book covering just the museums of England (fortunately, I had one of those with me on my trips).<br /><br />Don't think Lonely Planet's \\"England\\" is an armchair travel guide. Since it has so few photos, you won't find yourself leafing through it, spotting a stunning color picture and saying, \\"Wow, that looks like a great place to visit.\\" But, in my opinion, it IS one of the best guidebooks you can have when you're actually IN England. It's as complete a portrait of the country, its people and its activities as any I've seen. If you're an adventurous, curious, independent traveler, you should make sure it's in your suitcase or backpack on your next trip \\"across the pond.\\"specific regions of England. B005F9TQ9C|5.0|SELF PORTRAIT is rapidly becoming my favorite Bob Dylan album. That's an honest statement, yet in the context of my life it still sort of shocks me saying it, because in my time as a student, this was the most maligned of all of Bob Dylan's albums, at least by the hipper critics of the era (most notably in Rolling Stone).<br /><br />It was savaged by a lot of reviewers, mostly because it not only didn't conform to their expectations of what Dylan should have been doing (no doubt, from their point-of-view, writing the soundtrack for the revolution to come . . . .), but ran completely counter to who he seemed to be, in their eyes.<br /><br />It is laid back in tone and demeanor, even the live tracks with the Band from the 1969 Isle of Wight Festival, whether he's doing folk, country, pop, or blues (even some New Orleans stylings), and also features lots of covers of other people's songs, including perhaps the best rendition of \\"Take A Message To Mary\\" that I've ever heard (with all due respect to the Everly Brothers). It's taken a while, but I've even come to enjoy (especially on CD) the live rendition of \\"Like A Rolling Stone\\" from that Isle of Wight performance, which is a good showcase for the group as well.<br /><br />And it's all done with a simple, beguiling joy that comes from having unambitious (but richly textured) fun, albeit fun with a purpose. For SELF PORTRAIT is all about, essentially, Dylan reclaiming his own identity, musically and in popular culture, and demystifying himself in the process -- in effect, telling the world in 1970 that this is the stuff I like, it's where I came from musically (as much as Woody Guthrie -- already represented on his earlier output -- was), and that I want to do, and I'm not interested in anyone's opinion of it. Take it or leave it.<br /><br />A lot of critics (and some fans) couldn't accept that, and hated him for it, though the record did do quite well commercially at the time, charting high on both sides of the Atlantic (especially for a premium-priced double LP -- in the rock world of 1970, $15-$22 retail was a LOT of money even for a double album; and in the latter category, only the Beatles, the Cream, and the Who had really gotten away with charging that much at the time, with the \\"White Album,\\" Wheels Of Fire, and Tommy, respectively -- and the Cream's management had hedged their bets in England by splitting that album into two separate volumes); and it has always been in demand as a used LP, which tells you that Dylan was correct in his estimation of how unimportant the rock critics really were, at least where his music was concerned.<br /><br />But in terms of the record itself there's mostly stuff here to love in the listening, even really adore in the free-ranging nature of the sounds, from gospel to blues to pop (any album that linked Dylan's talent with that of Richard Rodgers and Lorenz Hart, via \\"Blue Moon,\\" deserved points for sheer daring in 1970 -- and Lord knows what Richard Rodgers thought of the recording itself, if he ever heard it). And he was leading the way, as usual, for a lot of other icons from the 1960s -- John Lennon and Paul McCartney abandoning the ambition, perfection and bold production of the Beatles' music, each for something more basic (and, in Lennon's case, bitterly personal, which probably, ultimately helped get him killed by attracting the mentally unwrapped and unstuck), the Moody Blues giving up on being the \\"world's smallest symphony orchestra\\" or pop mystics extraordinaire and declaring themselves to be just singers in the rock 'n' roll band . . . the big post-1960s demystifying of rock music (alas, mostly into a big business, except for those who chose to stay small) starts here.<br /><br />Listening to it in 2013, I suddenly realized -- the 1960s and its music (including Dylan's) liberated a lot of us. And SELF PORTRAIT was Dylan declaring his own liberation, in all of its laid back joy. I only wish they'd do a Deluxe version of this,perhaps with the outtakes that ended up on the 1973 \\"Dylan\\" album and the Isle of Wight tracks complete (or, at least, the ones here assembled together).<br /><br />And I thank Bob Dylan every day for releasing this record -- as I said, it's rapidly becoming my favorite of all of Bob Dylan's albums. And it may be, if my interpretation is correct, also the first actual Bob Dylan album (other than his debut), representing who Bob Dylan REALLY was, behind the public mask.perhaps with the outtakes that ended up on the 1973 \\"Dylan\\" album and the Isle of Wight tracks complete (or, at least, the ones here assembled together).<br /><br />And I thank Bob Dylan every day for releasing this record -- as I said, it's rapidly becoming my favorite of all of Bob Dylan's albums. And it may be, if my interpretation is correct, also the first actual Bob Dylan album (other than his debut), representing who Bob Dylan REALLY was, behind the public mask.

193700788X|4.0|A lot of people here are disappointed in the ending of the series.  I am not one of them.  I've read all the books (started before the series on HBO)and I've always viewed the world of the novels as distinctly different from the world on True Blood.<br /><br />SPOILERS***************************************<br />If you hadn't figured out that Sookie was going to end up with Sam, you weren't paying attention or you are a Truebie in need of an intervention.  It was clear from book 1 that Sookie could not end up with a vampire unless she herself became one, and that was never going to happen.  The nighttime world of vamps is not for our Sookie who loves sunbathing and wants children.  I thought it was always clear that Sookie would end up with someone living, and that meant Sam, Alcide, or Quinn.  For various reasons Alcide and Quinn weren't the men for her.  Sam, on the other hand, has always had great chemistry with Sookie.  Besides the fireworks (hinted at but never detonated), they are best friends, which anyone who has been married for a long time will tell you is essential to a lifelong relationship.<br /><br />Now, if you are a show watcher late to the books, you might have trouble envisioning Sam as anyone other than Sam Trammell.  Side note - I think he is poorly cast, as he looks and acts nothing like Sam in the books.  I always envisioned someone more like Aaron Eckhart or Kevin McKidd playing Sam.  I can understand the disappointment of Sookie ending up with show Sam, but book Sam has always been sexier and more stable.  You know the two of them will run Merlottes, have several children and grow old together as mainstays of the Bon Temps community.<br /><br />I did have a couple of issues with the book, the biggest one being the last minute appearance of Claude.  I've always had a problem with the fae storyline, and Claude's cameo was a little too deus ex machina for me.  I didn't need to see Quinn again either, although I understood that throwing him in gave a sense of closure for the Quinn/Sookie holdouts.  I've also had ongoing troubles with Mr. Cataliades since it was revealed that he can read minds and is the reason Sookie is a telepath.  If he could read minds, why did Sookie have to go to Rhodes back in All Together Dead?  Mr. C went from being an intriguing character to a useful plot device and that has always bothered me.<br /><br />I was never a huge fan of Eric and Sookie; I know that puts me in the minority, but I have known and dated men like Eric (selfish, power hungry, sexy but self-centered) and so the character didn't turn me on.  I prefer gentlemen, so I liked Bill and I like Sam.  The ending of Sookie's relationship with Eric was quick and brutal, but sometimes that's how it works.  There truly is no good way to break up with someone, and the fact of it is Eric wanted to be powerful and perhaps eventually a king more than he wanted Sookie. Also, he would have eventually turned her because he never could understand not wanting to be a vampire.<br /><br />All in all I thought it was a nice ending to a fun series.  I will miss spending time with Sookie.  I always felt like the books were a little vacation to Bon Temps, a chance to spend some time with people who were quirky but charming.  I will miss them, but at least I can always reread the books.

193700788X|5.0|I read literally hundreds of books each year, but this is the first review I have ever written. This is because I think written word can mean and be interpreted differently by different people. This is an important part of why reading and good story writing is so important. I did, in this case, feel compelled to defend this book, this series and this author.<br /><br />I have loved this series. For those whom felt there was no foreshadowing, I believe they were only \\"hearing\\" what they wanted to \\"hear\\" while reading. I felt that this would be the only appropriate ending to this series since reading the first book.  Sookie had said the times she had kissed Sam that the only reason she didn't pursue him was because he was her boss and she didn't want to change the dynamic of that relationship. As a real person, I can respect and agree with that sentiment. She was trying to be smart about it and not ruin a job that was her only source of income in a small town. I don't know if many of you have been in that position or can imagine what that would be like. She had no other options, or very few, as far as career. She didn't want to have a relationship, despite her feelings, becuase as we all know some relationships don't last. Then where would she be? With a house and life to live and no income. She now has financial security and a say in the business. This allows her to be more comfortable with any possible outcome of their relationship. Every time they were together intimately, there were fireworks. If you read again, you may see it differently... As far as doing things \\"true to character\\" I don't see how this could have been any truer to their characters. We all go through periods of growth or periods of time when we make some decisions that we may have made differently had we looked at them with our \\"true character\\" those are the things we end up hopefully learning from.  Sookie may have spent a few years in her 20's chasing around with dangerous, sexy men (which manyother people try out :) in real life), but that was never the person she was or her goal for her life. Her ending up with Eric or Bill would have been the least \\"true to character\\" thing that could have happened. Eric may have loved Sookie, but only as much as Eric can ever love anyone that is not himself. Yes, he was dynamic, funny and an absolute blast to know. That does not mean he was a truly reliable or steadfast being to his core. I know a lot of people in real life who are bigger than life and awesome to be around, but I wouldn't be leaving them my children to raise if I died or trust them with my whole heart or deepest secrets.  He was always going to do what was best for number 1, himself. That is why Sookie never trusted him completely and was waiting for the other shoe to drop. She loved him, but he was never to be her one great love. Sookie kept closest people who were good to their core, reliable, hard working and who appreciated the good/simple things in life. Bill's personality may have been closer to that, but were she a real person, she NEVER would have stayed with a vampire. This was too far from her core values and goals. Her home was Bon Temps, her only remaining family and her core support group, all in Bon Temps. This is what was meant to happen, people and if you can't see that, then you are the one who truly didn't understand Sookie or the other characters in the book, not the author.<br /><br />I did feel in certain parts of the books that certain themes or story lines were forced or rushed, but it is already a 13 book series. Some things had to be summarized and delivered quickly or it would be 30 books and then you would all really be complaining when it didn't end just the way you wanted!! (I would have been happy to read 30..) The great thing about a great storyteller is that they can take you along on a journey that you never could have thought of or dreamed up yourself. Charlaine truly is able to do that. I applaud her for that and admire her talent. My only wish for this final book is that it would have gone a little farther into the future and into tying things up, but it looks like CH may be planning on doing that this fall with a kind of tie it all up Sookie summary?? Maybe I read that wrong though somewhere. I would be happy to read one last look into the future of Sookie and hope that is the case.<br /><br />Remember to relax, enjoy and escape when you read. If you find yourself always looking for problems or discrepancies in movies, books or other forms of entertainment, you will never be truly entertained. I can't stand it when people watch movies and say \\"did you see/hear that? That would never happen.\\"  Isn't that quite the point?

How did people think they would be together if Charlaine Harris had decided to keep Eric as her lover?  She would continue aging unless she agreed to become a vampire.  She never wanted to become one.  She sees this side of Eric and realizes that she was in love with someone she didn't know.  I can understand her wish to hurry it along to get Eric out of the picture to live his live with his new queen.<br /><br />Bill - After Sookie discovers Bill's betrayal a few books back in how he came to be in her life, she was able to forgive him, but she never had the same feelings for him.  She remembered her love for him since he was her first, but it was never portrayed that she would get back together with him.  They became good friends.  Again, same problem with Eric, she would age if she got back together with him, and she didn't want to become a vampire.<br /><br />Sam - OK, he wasn't portrayed as a love interest to Sookie in the past few books, but he was always there when she needed him.  I think she started to care for him slowly.  So slow, I don't think she even realized what was happening until he died and she used the gift to bring him back to life.  Being brought back to life is life changing.  Sam started thinking about what matters and realized he had the best thing in his life already, Sookie.<br /><br />I don't understand all of the 1 star reviewers hatred for the book.  It almost seems like its planned to make this book a low rating.  One reviewer even sent out a request for everyone to put in a one star review.  A couple of people put in a low review and they haven't even read the book yet.  Where is the sense in that?  OK, I understand, she didn't end up with Eric, but really, if you really knew Sookie, why would she end up with him?  He couldn't live in the sun with her, he couldn't give her children, she didn't want to become a vampire to join him.  Love doesn't always conquer all. For those who wanted her to go back with Bill, where in the last few books showed any signs of that happening?  They had their memories of the love the shared, but I think they both grew up and were past that.<br /><br />The version of this book I had was the audio version (great series in audio-narrator is fabulous).  Listening to it, I guess I got more out of the book than from reading it.  I got emotional on Sookie's low point of the book and was glad she had friends and family to help her get back on her feet.  Anyway, that's all for now.  Bring on the bashing comments for not giving this a low rating.

Yeah, I know not every book should end \\"happily-ever-after,\\" but there just seemed to be too many disappointments in the book; those things that you'd really, really like to see turn out all right, but sadly don't. Just sayin'......<br /><br />So in conclusion, I enjoyed this book, and I think that many others will too, but I can't help but say that the writing felt a little weak. If you are very familiar with Star Trek: TNG, and the backstories of Data and Dr. Noonien Soong, then I'm sure you'll love this book. But if you aren't, I'd suggest looking for another book to read.
