She seems to be behind a grey veil.  For awhile I wondered if the CD had changed her as she speculated it had changed Sam, but she was back for awhile in Chapter 9, the same girl we always knew.  Her sudden shift from proclamations of love and incessant thoughts about one character then jumping in bed with another character about 10 days later are also out of character.  In previous books she always took much more time between relationships.  She indulges in some sex that can only be described as trailer trash, Jane Bodehouse-style, D-list porn.  Then she claims it is the best she ever had and that she may be with this character by Christmas...or maybe not.<br /><br />Frankly, all of this is just jarring and serves no purpose to the book at all.  The foul language and blatant discrimination are repulsive and also serve no purpose to the book.  There are many other ways this character could have been written and the plot driven in this book. Any good editor should have and would have caught this.  (This is a lesson in why you never employ friends or fans.)  The main character as a result becomes a complete failure in her last hoorah which is sad to say the least.<br /><br />The use of 3rd person omniscient is not used for any useful purpose here.  The prologue would have sufficed for the mystery.  After that it was unnecessary and was incredibly intrusive.  It felt like the author was saying, \\"Oh you didn't get it so let me hammer it into you.\\"  As if anyone who had read the books wouldn't recognize who was reading the bible or listening to his old sermons. (Not a spoiler as the prologue was published before.)  A better use of 3rd person would be to enter the minds of those other significant supporting characters who have been around for so long taking up much of the books.  She could have spent the time shedding light on their thoughts and motivations rather than shining light on an already obvious plotline.<br /><br />Last the entire book feels like it was put together with a shoe horn.  Different sub-plots seem completely unrelated and contrived to just get us to the end.  There is so much telling rather than showing that it is insulting.  By the end you will feel as if the author \\"schooled\\" you rather than showed you the story through Sookie's eyes.  There are blatant consistency errors.  A perfect example of this is when Sookie claims she cannot read supe minds.  She has read Eric and Alcide's minds very clearly.  She reads Sam's mind in the short story If I Had A Hammer which Harris herself says comes after the books.  This as so many other things is contrived blatantly to get her to an ending that she claims she wanted from book 2.  In all honesty the wind down of Sookie's previous relationship could have and should have been taken care of two books ago (one at the least).  It would have made sense then and left enough time to build to the second relationship.  Instead the second relationship is thrown on us out of the blue.  While the build up to Sookie's desire for normalcy and children was built over the last book and first third of this one, her feelings beyond friendship for the man in her next relationship are not.  As a result, this relationship seems very forced and unconvincing.  This will be a let down for any fans of that pairing.  There are still other blatant consistency errors that seem to be for the sake of shoving in the ending she wants, but they are just too numerous to mention.<br /><br />This book is an onerous snarl of a beast that offers no enjoyment.  Harris' Southern characters always reminded me a bit of Faulkner despite the fact that she lacked his writing skills.  After this book, I am left with a line Faulkner quotes in the beginning of The Sound and The Fury that describes Sookie's narration and her last tale precisely:<br /><br />it is a tale<br />Told by an idiot, full of sound and fury,<br />Signifying nothing. (Shakespeare, Macbeth, Act V, Scene v)

B001PTG4BO|3.0|There used to be a way of looking at the production of things. You would hone your craft in the hopes that a person who was really good at hawking well-honed crafts would agree to do that for you. This worked out great because you got to continue working on your craft, and you got the revenue from it (well, some of it, at least). The downside was that you had to rely on the advice of the hawker regarding what would sell. In effect, the hawkers - because they were a proxy for the customers - became the honers' only customer. What they felt they could sell became what could be sold. Let's call this the \\"agent-system\\".<br /><br />It replaced another system, in which you honed your craft in the hopes that an insanely rich person would see it and basically contract to buy everything you ever made ever again for ever and ever amen. 1455869910|5.0|I was skeptical about reading this novel, because generally I find it hard to relate to 'teenager' books. Firstly, this is not a 'teen' book, although it is good for teenagers/young adults to read as well. I decided to read it because I loved the title. It is inspired by Shakespeare's words in Julius-Cesar (acknowledged by the author),\\"The fault, dear Brutus is not in our stars, but in ourselves.\\" It is a beautifully written story of a seventeen year old girl Hazel, battling cancer.  The strength of this novel lies not only in the author's capability of instilling life into his characters, but also creating happiness despite the pathos that underlies the plot. As an oncologist, I also applaud John Green's deep understanding of the many levels of pain that cancer brings, and the different kinds of strength we surprisingly find in its victims. This book made me laugh, made me cry, kept me awake. It was light and easy to read while being deeply philosophical about life, death and love.<br />My favorite quotes are:<br />About love,\\"As he read, I fell in love the way you fall asleep: slowly and then all at once.\\"<br /><br />About cancer:\\"My dad understood my cancer the way I did: in the vague and incomplete way people understand electrical circuits and ocean tides. But my mom knew more about differentiated thyroid carcinoma in adolescents than most oncologists.\\"<br /><br />(Edited to avoid spoilers)\\"Some war. What am I at war with? My cancer. And what is my cancer? My cancer is me....It is a civil war..with a predetermined winner.\\"<br /><br />\\"Even cancer isn't a bad guy, really. Cancer is just trying to stay alive.\\"<br /><br />About loss,\\"Grief doesn't change you. It reveals you.\\"<br /><br />The acclaim this book has won is well-deserved.

193700788X|2.0|So I'm not sure how to write this review. Like most fans of Charlaine Harris's Sookie Stackhouse/Southern Vampire Mysteries/True Blood books, I was sad to find out that the most recent book, Dead Ever After, would be the last in the series but also like most fans of the books, I was pretty sure it was also time to wrap up the series because the last few books have really been disappointing and unfortunately, this one follows suit as well. The first 3/4 of the book was totally boring. It basically was chalk full of the daily goings on of Miss Stackhouse. The only bright spots were the numerous cameo appearances from all of your favorite characters from all the previous books. I honestly don't think anyone was left out. It almost felt as though Charlaine went down the character list and wrote every single one in to say goodbye. This day in and day out stuff continues until she is unjustly arrested for a murder. Then you see all the core characters come together to show their support for Sookie. In the meantime, Eric is brushed away and his story is wrapped up as well, all to make room for Sam to court Sookie. Just so we're clear, I've always been on Team Bill and always will be but with my personal opinions aside, I am not sure how I feel about the Sam/Sookie pairing. All through the books, except for the first one, Sam has always been her rock but always a friend and (for me) there has never been any sort of heat or passion between them. I know in the first couple books, Sam made some noise about wanting her and then he kissed her but Sookie always made it clear that there was nothing besides friendship between them. I kinda feel like Charlaine knew that she needed to give Sookie a HEA with one of the many men that have come into her life and Sam seemed to be the safest choice without pissing off too many fans. I guess, the series just lost its sparkle after about the 5th book and this was just the book to finish off the dying horse. There is a little surprise actionat the end but honestly, for being the last in the series, I expected a lot more but I guess after this book, I'm glad it's over.

0393343405|5.0|but hardly a demonstration of the subtitle: How the World BECAME MODERN.  Reading of one of the heroes Poggio's (the other Lucretius) life in the midst of both a very decadent Church and similarly decadent times gives fuel to an understanding of the Reformation.  If Poggio's reputed mistress and 12 children, whom he abandoned when he married in late middle age, were not just the typical calumnies that he and others hurled at each other, then despite his interest in recovering old manuscripts, he and his cohort were not very nice people. A time of false popes, corrupt curial politics, arrogance, use of theology for power, how is it possible to assert that his discovery of De Rerum Natura, about which he seemingly held no opinion was the fulcrum on which the world tipped toward becoming \\"modern?\\"  Based on anything the scientific revolution would call evidence, Lucretius' assertion of atomism is as speculative as a flat earth, god's creation in 6 days, the virgin birth and the triune god.  The Swerve does not fit with observations of the universe by any thinker relying on physical evidence since Ptolemy.<br /><br /> The author's list of Lucretius' modern claims is indeed impressive. It not only anticipates cosmological refutations of an anthropic universe but touches on Darwinism and even the Buddhist idea of emptiness.  But none of that is what Lucretius meant.  His context had few of the ideas of later science.  His Epicureanism could not conceive of endorphins shared both by humans and other animals.  He concepts, as the author points out, were \\"essentially erotic\\".  So while his words sound modern it is hard to say how they connect to the science that emerged after him and began to change the way the physical world is understood.  Copernicus, Galileo and finally Newton could be Christians and yet base their novel ideas, not on the particularly untestable idea of atoms, but on replicable observations. Newton's particles fit with his observations of mass and light.  They were not snatched out of philosophic speculation.  In John Heilbron's biography of Galileo Lucretius is not mentioned and atomism only four times, one of which was as an accusation by his prosecutors.  The Reformation turned the Inquisition into a paranoid, bloody  McCarthyism which blew even the slightest offense way out of proportion.  Atomism was hardly the greatest of sins.  And, as the author points out, Luther, Calvin and their ilk were no less zealous of Christian doctrine.<br /><br />My sense of the tipping point of the modern world lay not in humanist reorientation of speculative thinking but in the accumulation of practical knowledge from what we might call engineering problems.  This built slowly and out of sight of theologians and essay writers. Artillery, mining, architecture, alchemy, astrology/astronomy, navigation and the calendar, etc.  Poggio just missed da Vince but what inspired the latter must have been rife in Poggio's world.  Despite Poggio's discovery, he set no precedents for what was to come.<br /><br />Besides the possible inaccuracy of the book's claim, it is none the less a great picture of Poggio's world.  Poggio and his peers acerbic dialogues with some interlocutors taking positions contradicting established doctrine was what got Galileo in so much trouble.  Because the Inquisition did not yet have Protestants to battle, Poggio's generation got away with titling swords at doctrine. By the time of Giordano Bruno and Galileo such provocation could prove deadly.<br /><br />Poggio's antiquarianism, his essays, the politics of religion etc. leave us with an interesting picture of a form of life, which despite its plagues and petty intrigues, may not be much different than our own.  We get outraged by a blow-job in the Oval office.  Creationists abhor Darwinism.  The titillating descriptions of passion by Lucretius, Poggio and others don't hold a candle to internet pornography.  And it is interesting to try to separate out the obsession with sex from the Epicurean idea of moderation.  People still argue about god and purposeless materialism.  Not much new, but the author's presentation of an incarnation four or five hundred years old made for fascinating reading. When I told my brother-in-law that I was bored with reading he suggested this book.  He was right. It is very interesting. Its author, I assume, a humanist, exercised that wonderful perspective on the world.<br /><br />Charlie Fisher, author of \\"Dismantling Discontent: Buddha's Way Through Darwin's World\\" and the forthcoming \\"Meditation in the Wild: Buddhism's Origin in the Heart of Nature.\\"on with sex from the Epicurean idea of moderation. And what about Aspen, her childhood sweetheart and now a member of the royal guard?<br /><br />I truly enjoyed Kiera Cass's THE SELECTION, and especially loved returning to the series with THE ELITE. It's just so much fun!<br /><br />In this second of the trilogy, we do get a bit more information about Illea and its history, why things are the way they are and how they came to be that way. Maxon shows America a secret room in the palace that's filled with books, including the diaries of their founder.<br /><br />There's also more friction between America and Maxon (and Aspen) as America continues to wrestle with her feelings for the prince and the competition as a whole. Cass walks a fine line between America becoming just a little too wishy washy, but I think she backed off and/or added enough explanation at the right time to make this a believable situation for me.<br /><br />I'm anxiously awaiting the third installment. I still want to know so much more about Illea and the rebels. And I can't wait to see who will choose who in the end!
B00BQ1DBUS|5.0|Ok...I usually frown on 5 star reviews because I think I have a different idea of what a 5 star recording should sound like or compare to...like albums like Abbey Road Pet Sounds or Rumours...that being said...Time is NOT Rod Stewart's best record...not even close. B00A81MV3U|4.0|Note: May contain spoilers. Proceed with caution.<br /><br />So I finally got around to watching Silver Linings Playbook, the movie that everyone's been talking about, and I must say that I was pretty impressed. It's not perfect, but it has a lot of heart and I honestly can't remember seeing a movie this emotionally driven. It deals with some tough issues, mainly mental instability, and it does so with a degree of classiness that transforms this from a movie about the troubled to an emotional story about the changes people face on a day to day basis. We always want to go back to what we know, but sometimes that's not always what's best for us and we need to learn to move on.<br /><br />The story follows a young man by the name of Pat Solitano Jr. (Bradley Cooper) as he's released from a mental health facility. Eight months ago he caught his wife cheating on him and that's when he found out that he was suffering from a bipolar disorder. In an attempt to stay out of prison after beating his wife's lover half to death, he settled on a plea bargain with the courts allowing him to attend this mental facility to better himself. After his mom picks him up from the facility he creates a plan. He's going to do a lot of reading and exercising in order to keep his mind in a solid place, but he has a major roadblock. He can't let go of his wife.<br /><br />After having dinner at his friend Ronnie's (John Oritz) house, Pat meets Tiffany Maxwell (Jennifer Lawrence). Tiffany is a recent widow who just lost her job. She suffers from extreme depression which she handles by sleeping around with random people to help fill that void. She strikes up a deal with Pat when she tells him that she can get his letter to his wife. He would do it himself, but she had a restraining order put on him. In return for her help Pat agrees to assist her with this upcoming dance competition. What starts as two people helping each other soon turns into a deal filled with lies and deceit as the viewer learns that one of the parties involved may not be as helpful as they led you to believe. It's not done with ill intention so it feels fitting given the situation of both of these individuals.<br /><br />As a whole I felt the story was solid. I think the main focus was on these troubled individuals who are looked at as psychos in the \\"real\\" world when really they're just like everyone else. They're people struggling through life just like the rest of us. So I thought it was commendable when both the leads and a few of the supporting characters all had some sort of mental problem. What makes matters worse for these characters is that people do look at them like some sort of circus show. Whenever Pat would have an episode there was this kid who kept coming to the house to see if he could record it for \\"a school project\\". It makes you empathize with Pat and it leaves you feeling disgusted that there are people like that in the world.<br /><br />Everything in the story leads up to the dance competition and that's where I felt the film got a little rocky. It felt more like a means to an end than something substantial and I kind of felt like it derailed the mood set by the film's previous hour and 40 minutes. It's very loud and fun while majority of the movie is dark and somber. I understood its purpose in the big picture, but I think there may have been a better way to reach the point the movie sets out to make.<br /><br />Silver Linings Playbook is led by Cooper and Lawrence who both gave excellent performances. Cooper proves once again that he's beyond being the eye-candy in The Hangover films and that he's an actor who everyone should take seriously. This was actually my introduction to Ms. Lawrence and I thought she gave a solid performance. I can't say that I understand all the hype around her just yet, and I don't understand how she won an award over Cooper, but she has a lot of potential. I just feel like she's still a developing actress with a lot of room to grow.<br /><br />On top of the leads you have Robert De Niro playing Pat Senior, Jackie Weaver as Pat's mom, Chris Tucker as Pat's friend Danny, John Ortiz as Ronnie, and Anupam Kher as Pat's therapist Cliff. I said earlier that a few of the supporting characters had mental issues as well and those two characters would be Danny and Pat Sr. I don't remember if they specified what Danny's condition was, but Pat Sr. is a very superstitious individual suffering from OCD and he's also developed a slight gambling problem after losing his job. Out of the supporting cast I think De Niro gave the most memorable performance. He's always been able to tackle almost any role he's given and this was no different. With that said, everyone involved did a great job and it was nice to see Tucker on the big screen again.<br /><br />Finally, the movie suffers from a few minor inconsistencies. The most obvious being when Pat and Tiffany are at the dance. When they start walking up the stairs Pat's on the right and Tiffany's on the left, but once they reach the top they're randomly switched. I don't know about you, but I've never seen two people walking up a set of stairs holding hands only to switch places along the way. Then during one of the practices Pat's necklace is in the front of his shirt, but when frame cuts to Tiffany it's on his back. Lastly, I don't know if it was the lighting or what, but in some scenes Tiffany's hair looks red while in majority of the film it's black, or at least it looked black. Now none of this is major, but it's stuff that I noticed because of who I am.<br /><br />Overall Score: 8.75/10 - Silver Linings Playbook is one of those films that is just so easy to fall in love with. Between the characters and the script everything feels so comfortable and familiar. It's almost as if we know these characters in real life, they just have different faces. Cooper's performance, in particular, is phenomenal and he really brings out the heart and soul of his character andhe gave, what I think, is the performance of his career. While the movie may not be perfect, there's a lot to love here and I definitely recommend taking the time to watch it.

B009IEXKXI|5.0|This is without doubt the best sci-fi book written by an independent author I've read so far. I really enjoyed it from start to finish. It is the story of an astronaut stranded on Mars, presumed dead and left behind by the rest of the crew. Wounded and with few resources, Mark Watney, must find a way to survive in a desert and hostile planet.<br />The words with which the novel opens delineate the tone immediately. We are faced with the irony of a character that has nothing to lose and finds in himself, also thanks to its scientific expertise, the strength to fight for his own survival.<br />The story starts with an assumption which is not correct, i.e. a sandstorm has forced the crew to abort the mission. This is impossible given that the Martian atmosphere is so thin that, even in case of strong winds, these are not able to cause any damage, beside covering everything with powder. But this is fiction, not reality.<br />With the suspension of disbelief the story begins, and from that point on it is a succession of brilliant stunts. Mark is from time to time to face new challenges and find improbable, but not impossible solutions. At the same time you follow the action on Earth, at the mission control, where at the beginning the protagonist is believed to be dead. Here, too, the irony rules. Several times I laughed out loud while reading. The dialogues are withering. Same for the diary of Mark, which is at times hilarious, almost as if the protagonist, trying to deal with fear for his condition, jokes about it, according to a custom typical of American films and TV series.<br />The result is a really beautiful hard sci-fi novel.<br />Reading the reviews, I found out that the author has published it in serial form, and only in the end he released the entire novel. I must say that considering how the story is structured, with a number of problems that arise and are resolved, it actually looks episodic, but I admit that while reading I had not realized it at all.<br />The excellent work of Andy Weir, apparently, has been rewarded. The book was bought by a major publishing house and will be re-released in 2014. In the meantime, it is still available as audiobook.<br /><br />Rita Carla Francesca Monticelli, author of [[ASIN:B00L979374 Red Desert - Point of No Return]]The excellent work of Andy Weir, apparently, has been rewarded. B007VTVGFQ|5.0|This album is one of the few-ever perfect marriages of sophisticatedly profound poetics with virtuosic musical composition and performance. Even in trying to sum it up in one sentence, I trip over my own words like an idiot who can't pronounce the words he's using. I myself sprang for the \\"deluxe\\" edition with the comosition book of Ms. Apple's lyrics and sketches (plus the live-performance DVD). With or without it, this album is an overwhelming aesathetic experience, because of and in honor of which one might be disinclined to pay for or pay attention to any other music for a year's time. I can't even go into the details of the songs themselves - Ms. Apple's lyrics, piano playing and vocal delivery all close in on the songs like fingers into a fist, for each selection. I've read others' attempts to aptly describe or appropriately quote the songs on this album, and I find that almost everyone fails to approximate a listener's experience of the songs. Indeed, these songs aren't to be merely listened-to, but experienced by the listener; that this entire album is a comprehensive experience itself, making cherry-picking the selections which one can more comfortably digest seem a desecration of the entire work. Fiona Apple is as amazing as she is humble about it. A lot of musicians should follow her example on both counts.
